Objection: Bodily Power is Not Effective
Begins the objection that the proof's premises are not accepted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite objection that the proof's premise about bodily power being effective is not conceded.

Core Idea
Bodily power does not truly cause motion; only Allah creates effects.

This lesson presents the first objection to the philosophers' proof. The author states that the entire proof is built on premises that are denied. The first premise concerns bodily power being effective in motion—either naturally in its own body or forcibly in another. The Ash'arites reject this, arguing that in forced motion, the true agent is the coerced body's power subjugated to the coercer, not the coercer itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
Read the opening line: the proof is based on several matters, all denied. This sets up a list of objections, but only the first is given here.
The passage is incomplete; only the first matter is discussed.
2
Understand the first denied premise
The premise is that bodily power (al-quwwa al-jismaniyya) is effective either naturally in its own body or forcibly in another. Note the two types.
3
Grasp the Ash'arite analysis of forced motion
The author explains that in forced motion, the true effective agent is the power of the coerced body subjugated to the coercer, not the coercer himself. The coercer is only a preparer (mu'idd).
This challenges the common assumption that the coercer causes the motion.
4
Recognize the sectarian context
The phrase 'not conceded according to us' refers to the Ash'arites. The Mu'tazilites agree with the philosophers on this point, indicating a sectarian divide on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الدليل مبني على عدة أمور كلها ممنوعة : الأول : أن القوة الجسمانية مؤثرة تأثيراً طبيعياً في جسم هو محلها، أو قسرياً في جسم آخر، وذلك غير مسلم عندنا، بل قوله : (أو قسرياً في جسم (آخر) هذا بناء على ما هو المشهور، وأما في التحقيق فالمؤثر في القسرية قوة المقسور المسخرة للقاسر لا القاسر فإنه كالمعد لتلك الحركة. قوله : وذلك غير مسلم عندنا يعني الأشاعرة وأما المعتزلة الموافقون للحكماء في
And this proof is based on several matters, all of which are denied: The first: that bodily power is effective naturally in a body that is its substrate, or forcibly in another body. That is not conceded according to us, rather... He said: (or forcibly in another body) this is based on what is well-known; but in reality, the effective agent in forced motion is the power of the coerced body that is subjugated to the coercer, not the coercer himself, for he is like a preparer for that motion. He said: 'That is not conceded according to us' meaning the Ash'arites. As for the Mu'tazilites who agree with the philosophers in...
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage begins the author's objection to the previous proof. He states that the entire proof is built on several premises that are all rejected (mamnu'a). The first denied premise is that bodily power (al-quwwa al-jismaniyya) is effective—either naturally in its own body or forcibly in another body. The author says this is not conceded ('inda-na). The commentary explains that in forced motion, the true effective agent is the power of the coerced body that is subjugated (musakhkhara) to the coercer, not the coercer himself; the coercer is only a preparer (mu'idd). Then it clarifies that 'not conceded according to us' refers to the Ash'arite school, while the Mu'tazilites agree with the philosophers on this point. The passage is cut off, so the full objection is not given.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodily power is truly effective in causing motion naturally or forcibly.
Correction: Ash'arites deny this; only Allah creates effects. In forced motion, the coerced body's subjugated power is the agent, not the coercer.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite position stated here, what is the true agent in forced motion?
A) The coercer's power
B) The power of the coerced body subjugated to the coercer
C) The natural motion of the body
D) There is no agent

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arites reject that bodily power is genuinely effective, arguing that in forced motion the true agent is the coerced body's subjugated power, not the coercer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan an action, do you truly believe that your own power is the cause, or do you acknowledge that Allah is the real agent? How does this belief change how you approach your daily tasks? Commit to reciting 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' before your next task.
?
The coercer in the example thinks he is causing motion, but the Ash'arites say he is only a preparer. In your life, where do you act as a 'coercer'—thinking you are the cause of outcomes? How can you shift your reliance to Allah?
?
The Mu'tazilites agreed with philosophers on bodily power. Why might it be dangerous to attribute true causal power to creation? Examine your own heart: do you sometimes feel that your skills or efforts are independent of Allah? How can you cultivate tawakkul?

The Philosophers' Proof for the Finitude of Bodily Force
The author presents the philosophers' argument for finitude of motion from natural and forced force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers use a ratio argument to prove that bodily force is finite, both in natural and forced motion.

Core Idea
Bodily force is proven finite because infinite motion leads to contradiction with partial motions.

The philosophers present a clever logical argument to demonstrate that any force residing in a body must be finite. They consider both natural motion (where the body moves due to its own nature) and forced motion (where it is pushed by another). By assuming that a force could produce infinite motion, they derive a contradiction using the relationship between a whole and its part (or double). This proof is a cornerstone of the philosophical view that all bodily forces are limited.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of motion
The argument covers natural motion (body moves by its own nature) and forced motion (body moved by an external coercer). Recognize these two cases.
Natural motion is intrinsic; forced motion requires an external cause.
2
Follow the natural motion argument
Assume a natural force moves its body infinitely. Consider half of that body: it has half the force and thus half the motion. If both start from the same point, the half's motion must be half the whole's motion.
This sets up a ratio: force is proportional to motion.
3
Follow the forced motion argument
Assume a coercive force moves another body infinitely. Then the same force could move double that body, producing half the motion. Again, starting from the same point, the double's motion is half the half's motion.
The same ratio logic applies: double the mass yields half the motion.
4
Analyze the lesser and greater motions
In both cases, we compare the lesser motion (half in natural, double in forced) and the greater motion (the assumed infinite). Either the lesser is finite or infinite. Each possibility leads to a contradiction.
If lesser is finite, greater is finite (contradiction). If lesser is infinite, greater is greater than infinite (impossible).
5
Conclude the finitude of bodily force
Since assuming infinite motion leads to contradiction, bodily force cannot produce infinite motion. Therefore, all bodily forces are finite.
The proof does not rely on empirical observation but pure logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في القوة الجسمانية
المقدمة
في الحركة الطبيعية والثانية في الحركة القسرية ( فإذا فرضناهما) أي التحريك الطبيعي والقسري ( من مبدأ واحد ) أي فحينئذ نقول : لا يجوز أن تحرك قوة طبيعية جسمها إلى غير النهاية وإلا فنصف ذلك الجسم له قوة طبيعية هي نصف القوة الطبيعية التي للكل فنفرض أن هاتين القوتين حركتا جسميهما من مبدأ واحد في العدد أو الزمان فلا شك أن حركة النصف نصف حركة الكل؛ لما مر في الأولى . وكذلك نقول : لا يجوز أن تكون قوة جسمانية تحرك جسماً آخر بالقسر إلى غير النهاية، وإلا فلذلك القاسر أن يحرك ضعف ذلك الجسم الآخر، فنفرض أنه حركهما من مبدأ واحد فلا شك أن حركة الضعف نصف حركة النصف لما مر في المقدمة الثانية، فإذا فرضنا ما ذكرنا في الطبيعية والقسرية (فالأقل) وهو حركة النصف في الطبيعية وحركة الضعف في القسرية (إما متناه والأكثر) الذي فرضناه غير متناه ( ضعفه ) ؛ لما عرفت . ( وضعف المتناهي متناه) بالضرورة، فيكون الأكثر متناهياً، وهو خلاف المفروض، وإما غير متناه، وقد فرضنا مبدأ الأقل والأكثر ( واحداً ، ( فتقع الزيادة عليه ) أي زيادة الأكثر على الأقل ) في الجهة التي هو بها غير متناه، فهو متناه إذ لا بد أن ينقطع في تلك الجهة حتى تتصور الزيادة عليه فيها، ( وأنه ) أي كون الأقل متناهياً في الجهة التي هو فيها غير متناه ( محال ) بالضرورة .
The Fifth Observation - The Fifth Intention: The statement of the philosophers concerning bodily force. The introduction. [It is] about natural motion and second about forced motion. (Then if we assume them both) meaning the natural and forced causing of motion (from a single starting point) meaning then we say: It is not permissible that a natural force moves its body to infinity, otherwise then half of that body has a natural force that is half of the natural force of the whole. So we assume that these two forces moved their two bodies from a single starting point in number or time. There is no doubt that the motion of the half is half the motion of the whole, as was passed in the first [premise]. Similarly we say: It is not permissible that a bodily force should move another body by force to infinity, otherwise then that coercer can move double that other body. So we assume that it moved them both from a single starting point. There is no doubt that the motion of the double is half the motion of the half, as was passed in the second premise. So when we assume what we mentioned in natural and forced [cases], (the lesser) which is the motion of the half in the natural and the motion of the double in the forced (is either finite and the greater) which we assumed infinite (its double) as you have known. (And the double of the finite is finite) necessarily, so the greater becomes finite, and that is contrary to the assumption. Or it is infinite, and we have assumed the starting point of the lesser and greater (one). (Then the increase upon it occurs) i.e., the increase of the greater over the lesser in the direction in which it is infinite, so it is finite, since it must be cut off in that direction for the increase upon it to be conceivable. (And that) i.e., that the lesser is finite in the direction in which it is infinite (is impossible) necessarily.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' proof for the finitude of motion produced by bodily force, both natural and forced. The author first states the assumption: if we consider the natural and forced motions as starting from a single point, we can analyze the motion of the whole and a part. For natural motion, if a body's natural force were to move it an infinite distance, then half of that body would have half the force and thus half the motion. Assuming both start from the same point, the half's motion must be half of the whole's. If the whole's motion is infinite, the half's motion is either finite or infinite. If it is finite, then the whole's motion must be finite because the ratio of forces equals the ratio of motions, and the forces are in a finite ratio (half to whole). If the half's motion were infinite, then the whole's motion would be even greater, but since both start from the same point, the whole's motion would exceed the infinite half's motion in the direction of infinity—this is contradictory because nothing can exceed the infinite in that direction. The same reasoning applies to forced motion, where the coercing force moves a body an infinite distance; then it could move double that body, but the motion of the double must be half the motion of the half, again leading to a contradiction. Thus, the philosophers conclude that such infinite motion is impossible, proving that bodily force must be finite.

Key Terms
القوة الطبيعية
القوة القسرية
الحركة
التناهي
النسبة
المبدأ الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers assume infinite motion is possible.
Correction: The proof is by contradiction: they assume infinite motion to derive a contradiction, proving it is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the philosophers' proof for the finitude of bodily force?
A) The assumption that motion cannot begin without a cause
B) A comparison between the motion of the whole and the motion of a part, using the ratio of forces
C) The impossibility of infinite regress in causes
D) The observation that all bodies eventually stop moving

Key Takeaway
Bodily force must be finite because assuming infinite motion leads to contradictory ratios between whole and part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof demonstrates that every bodily force is limited. In what area of your life do you most often act as if your strength or energy is unlimited? How does this truth call you to rely fully on the One whose power is infinite?
?
If all created force is finite, then your ability to obey Allah or resist sin is also finite. Does this make you despair or drive you to seek His help? What specific act of worship will you ask Him to empower you for today, knowing you cannot do it alone?
?
The proof forces you to confront that even your best efforts have a limit. Are you content with your limits, or do you resent them? How can acknowledging your finitude free you to trust Allah's infinite generosity rather than your own performance?

Denying the First Premise: Bodily Force is Not Causal
Commentator denies the first premise and explains the proof and the response to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'aris deny that bodily force has independent causal efficacy and how this denial refutes the philosophers' proof for the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
Bodily forces do not cause effects; all causation is from Allah.

The philosophers' proof that the world is eternal relies on the premise that bodily forces produce effects naturally. The commentator, following Ash'ari doctrine, denies this premise entirely. In this lesson, we examine the first premise and the commentator's refutation, including an objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the first premise
Read the passage that states the first premise and the commentator's initial denial. Notice the distinction between natural and coercive effect.
The commentator says 'that is not granted by us'.
2
Understand the proof structure
The commentator outlines the philosophers' proof in detail, showing how they argue for infinite motion from a single starting point. Follow the logic of ratios.
The proof involves ratios of forces and motions.
3
Objection and answer
An objection is raised: if bodily force has no effect, then it is not described as non-finite, which is what the philosophers wanted. The answer explains that the negation is on the restriction (non-finiteness), not the effect itself.
This is a key subtlety.
4
Summary of first premise refutation
Confirm that denying the first premise blocks the proof because the proof depends on bodily forces being effective.

And this proof is based on several matters, all of which are denied: The first: that the bodily force is effective, naturally in a body that is its locus, or coercively in another body, and that is not granted by us, rather... [Commentary] Qawluhu: (Then we say, etc.) i.e., when assuming the two motions from a single starting point, we say: in detail concerning each one of them thus, and the summary of the proof concerning natural motion is that if a body moved by its natural force with infinite motions and a part of that body moved by its natural force from a single starting point, then if the motions of the part are infinite and the motions of the whole are more, variance occurs between the two motions in the infinite side; and if they are finite, the finitude of the motions of the whole also follows, because the ratio of the motion of the whole to the part is the ratio of the force of the whole to the force of the part, and the ratio of the two forces is like the ratio of the whole to the part, and their ratio is the ratio of the finite to the finite, so the ratio of the two motions is the ratio of the finite to the finite, and we have assumed the motion of the whole infinite, this is a contradiction. And analogize to that the proof of the coercive. Qawluhu: (As you have known that the ratio between the two effects is like the ratio between the two forces, and the ratio between them is like the ratio between the two bodies.) Qawluhu: (Or coercively in another body) this is based on what is well-known, but in verification the effective agent in coercion is the force of the coerced subjugated to the coercer, not the coercer, for it is like a preparation for that motion. Qawluhu: And that is not granted by us, meaning the Ash'aris; as for the Mu'tazila who agree with the philosophers in that all events are initially referred to God, may He be exalted. So if you say: If it is not effective at all, it is also described as non-finite in effect, and that is the desired [conclusion]. I say: The meaning of their statement is that it is effective with a finite effect, not infinite, and this desired [conclusion] whose proof is also dependent on its having a natural or coercive effect has no [validity]. (The second: that the half) of the body (has an effective force) and it is not necessary, because it is possible that a body has an effective force residing in it, and when that body is divided into halves, that force is entirely annihilated, just as the unity of that body is annihilated by division. So the half of the body has no force at all, and if it is assumed that it has a force that is a part of the force of the whole, it does not necessarily follow that it is a part of a force strong for action. For example, ten [people] if they lift a stone a distance, then one of them individually [cannot lift it]... [Commentary] Qawluhu: (It is not described as non-finite in effect, for the truth of our statement 'the bodily force does not produce an infinite effect' is either by denial of effect, or by realization of effect with denial of non-finiteness.) Qawluhu: (The meaning of their statement, etc.) means that the negation in their statement is directed to the restriction, which is non-finiteness, not to the restricted, i.e., the effect. Qawluhu: (That it is part of a force, etc.) for a part of a force does not necessarily have to be a force, because of the possibility of lack of similarity between the part and the whole in reality. [Then a passage about the Mu'tazila] They do not mention this denial and restrict themselves to what follows of the denials. Qawluhu: I say: The meaning of their statement that it is effective, etc.) The summary of the answer is that they claim the necessity of finitude of the apparent effect and the sensible order between bodily forces and effects, and that is not established on the assumption of denial of the original effect. Qawluhu: (When that body is divided into halves, that force is entirely annihilated) due to the extreme smallness of the locus. Then this denial is concerning natural force, but as for coercive force, it is said: The mover, when it moves a body coercively, it does not necessarily have the ability to move its double by half the motion of the half, but rather it may not move it at all. This is an explanation of what he mentioned, but there is discussion in it, because they have no need in conducting the proof to consider the division of that body, because it is possible to proceed in such a small locus by the method of duplication, by saying: If we assume another body whose magnitude is double the magnitude of this body for which we have posited an infinite effective force, then its force is double its force, and there is no doubt about the existence of a body whose force is double the force of this body. Then he conducted the speech to the other [premise], that it suffices the existence of a body whose force is greater than the force of the first body by a finite amount, and they have no need to prove a force that is double the force of the first body. Yes, the apparent [meaning] of what he mentioned is that force divides with division...
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation of the philosophers' proof by denying its premises. The commentator begins by stating that the proof is based on several matters, all of which are rejected. The first premise is that bodily force is effective, either naturally in its own locus or coercively in another body. The commentator notes that this is not granted by the Ash'aris. Then follows a lengthy commentary explaining the proof in detail (this commentary is part of the source text, included in the lesson). The key point: the effectiveness of bodily force is denied. The commentator then addresses an objection: if bodily force is not effective at all, then it is also not described as non-finite in effect—but that is exactly what the philosophers wanted to prove. The answer is that the philosophers' claim is that the force has a finite effect, not that it has no effect. The negation applies to the restriction (non-finiteness), not to the effect itself. The second premise is that half of a body has an effective force. This is denied because force may be annihilated when the body is divided, just as unity vanishes. Even if the half has a part of the whole's force, it is not necessarily a force that can act effectively (e.g., ten people lifting a stone vs. one person). The commentary then discusses further points: the Mu'tazila's position, the possibility of duplication instead of division in the proof, and a detailed analysis of the denial of the first and second premises. The overall conclusion is that the philosophers' proof fails because its premises are not granted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying the effectiveness of bodily force implies denying observable causation entirely.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that all causation is from God; bodily forces are merely occasions, not independent causes.

Denying the Second Premise: Half Does Not Retain Force
Commentator denies the second premise about half's force and provides supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that half of a body has effective force is not necessary, and how this further refutes the philosophers' proof.

Core Idea
Force does not necessarily survive division; a half may have no force at all.

The second premise of the philosophers' proof assumes that if a whole body has force, then half of it has an effective force as well. The commentator shows this is not necessary, because force may be annihilated upon division. This lesson examines the denial of the second premise and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise
Read the passage that states the second premise and the initial denial. Notice the analogy with the unity of the body.
The premise is denied because force may disappear when the body is divided.
2
Understand the example of ten people
The commentator gives an example: ten people can lift a stone, but one person alone cannot. This illustrates that a part does not necessarily have the same capability as the whole.
This shows that a part may lack effective force even if the whole has it.
3
Consider the coercive case
The discussion extends to coercive motion, where the coercive force may not be divisible.
This strengthens the denial.
4
Reflect on the alternative method of duplication
The commentator notes that the proof could be recast using duplication instead of division, but that still does not prove the premise.

[Commentary] Qawluhu: (Then we say, etc.) i.e., when assuming the two motions from a single starting point, we say: in detail concerning each one of them thus, and the summary of the proof concerning natural motion is that if a body moved by its natural force with infinite motions and a part of that body moved by its natural force from a single starting point, then if the motions of the part are infinite and the motions of the whole are more, variance occurs between the two motions in the infinite side; and if they are finite, the finitude of the motions of the whole also follows, because the ratio of the motion of the whole to the part is the ratio of the force of the whole to the force of the part, and the ratio of the two forces is like the ratio of the whole to the part, and their ratio is the ratio of the finite to the finite, so the ratio of the two motions is the ratio of the finite to the finite, and we have assumed the motion of the whole infinite, this is a contradiction. The First Objection: Does Denying Infinite Effect Deny All Effect?
Objection and answer about bodily force's effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari answer to the first objection distinguishes between negating the infinity of an effect and negating the effect entirely.

Core Idea
Denying infinite effect does not deny all effect.

In this passage, the author tackles a common objection against the Ash'ari position that bodily forces are not true causes. The objector claims that if forces have no effect at all, then they also cannot be described as having infinite effect—but that is precisely the conclusion the Ash'aris want. The answer clarifies a subtle distinction: the philosophers are only denying infinite effect, not effect as such.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the objection's structure and the concise answer.
2
Identify the key distinction
The answer says the negation in the philosophers' statement applies to the qualification 'infinite' not to 'effect'. Find where this is explained.
3
Understand the proof dependence
The objection's desired conclusion relies on the premise that forces have a natural or compulsory effect. See why this is problematic.
4
Reflect on the summary
The final part summarizes: the philosophers claim finitude of apparent effect, which is not established if original effect is negated. Grasp the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحوادث كلها مستندة إلى الله سبحانه ابتداءً فإن قلت : إذا لم تكن مؤثرة أصلاً توصف باللاتناهي في التأثير أيضاً، وهو المطلوب . قلت : معنى كلامهم أنها مؤثرة تأثيراً متناهياً لا غير متناه ولا ثبوت لهذا المطلوب الذي دليله أيضاً موقوف على أن لها تأثيراً طبيعياً أو قسرياً . قوله : (لم توصف باللاتناهي في التأثير فإن صدق قولنا : القوة الجسمانية لا تؤثر أثراً غير متناه إما بانتفاء التأثير، أو بتحقق التأثير مع انتفاء اللاتناهي . قوله : ( معنی کلامهم إلخ يعني أن النفي في قولهم متوجه إلى القيد، وهو اللاتناهي، لا إلى المقيد أعني التأثير. قوله : لهذا المطلوب الذي دليله إلخ) هذا الوصف لا دخل له في الجواب، وإنما ضمه لإيضاح أن هذا الدليل مبني على هذه المقدمة. قوله : ( أن يكون جزءاً لقوة إلخ) فإن جزء القوة لا يلزم أن يكون قوة لجواز عدم التشابه بين الجزء والكل في الحقيقة. إثبات القوى الطبيعية وتأثيرها حقيقة فهم لا يذكرون هذا المنع ويقتصرون على ما بعده من المنوع. قوله : قلت : معنى كلامهم أنها مؤثرة إلخ) حاصل الجواب : أنهم يدعون وجوب تناهي التأثير الظاهري والترتيب المحسوس الذي بين القوى الجسمانية والآثار وذلك لا يثبت على تقدير انتفاء أصل التأثير.
All events are initially dependent on God, exalted. If you say: if they (i.e., bodily forces) are not effective at all, they are described as not having infinite effect as well, which is the desired conclusion. I say: the meaning of their statement is that they (bodily forces) are effective with a finite effect, not infinite, and this desired conclusion has no proof, and its proof is also dependent on their having a natural or compulsory effect. His statement: (not described as having infinite effect) – for our statement 'bodily force does not produce an infinite effect' is true either by the negation of effect, or by the realization of effect with the negation of infinity. His statement: (meaning of their statement etc.) – meaning that the negation in their statement is directed to the qualification, which is infinity, not to the qualified, i.e., effect. His statement: (for this desired conclusion whose proof etc.) – this description has no bearing on the answer, but he included it to clarify that this proof is based on this premise. His statement: (that it be a part of a force etc.) – for a part of a force is not necessarily a force, due to the possibility of dissimilarity between the part and the whole in reality. The proof of natural forces and their real effect – they do not mention this prevention and limit themselves to what follows of preventions. His statement: (I say: meaning of their statement that they are effective etc.) – the summary of the answer: they claim the necessity of finitude of apparent effect and the sensible order between bodily forces and their effects, and that is not established on the supposition of the negation of the original effect.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the effect of bodily forces. The objection begins with the premise that all events are dependent on God initially. The objector argues: if bodily forces are not effective at all, then they are also not described as having infinite effect, and that is the desired conclusion (i.e., that they are not infinitely effective). The answer clarifies that the philosophers' claim is that bodily forces are effective but with a finite effect, not infinite. The desired conclusion has no proof because its proof depends on assuming that bodily forces have a natural or compulsory effect. The commentary then explains the nuances: the negation in the philosophers' statement pertains to the qualification of infinity, not to the effect itself. The additional description in the answer is to show that the proof depends on that premise. The part of a force is not necessarily a force itself, avoiding the fallacy of composition. The passage concludes by noting that the proof of natural forces' real effect does not mention this particular prevention (i.e., the objector's move) and instead limits itself to later preventions. The summary of the answer is that the philosophers claim the finitude of apparent effect and sensible order, which is not proven if the original effect is negated.

Key Terms
القوة الجسمانية
اللاتناهي
التأثير
المنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying infinite effect implies denying any effect at all.
Correction: The answer clarifies that the negation is only about infinity, not about the effect itself. Bodily forces can have a finite effect while lacking an infinite one.

Checkpoint
What is the key clarification in the answer to the first objection?
A) Bodily forces have no effect at all.
B) Bodily forces have an infinite effect.
C) Bodily forces have a finite effect, and the objection confused the negation of infinity with negation of effect.
D) Bodily forces are not real causes.

Key Takeaway
The objector's flaw is equating 'no infinite effect' with 'no effect at all,' but the Ash'aris affirm a finite effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed something as completely useless just because it was not perfect or infinite? Where in your life have you made the same logical error as the objector—equating 'not infinite' with 'none at all'? Will you stop that today and see the value of finite gifts?
?
This lesson shows that denying a quality (infinity) does not negate the thing itself (effect). Think of a relationship, skill, or worship you’ve neglected because it felt small or limited. Are you ready to affirm its finite but real value and act on it right now?
?
The objector built his conclusion on a premise the Ash'aris reject. What assumption are you holding onto that might be invalid? Will you question it today, even if it challenges your comfort?

Second Objection: Force of Half a Body? A Response
Sanusi states the second objection and gives two refutations with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that half a body must have an effective force is not necessarily true, and how the example of ten people lifting a stone illustrates the argument.

Core Idea
Force may disappear or become ineffective upon division—it does not automatically transfer to halves.

Sanusi presents the second objection: since the whole body has force, its half should also have force. But he refutes this by showing that force can vanish entirely when a body is divided, just as unity does. Even if force remains, it may be insufficient for action. Let's examine his argument and the illustrative example.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage that states the second objection: the half of a body has effective force. Note the wording.
The objection is phrased as a claim that might seem obvious.
2
Identify the first part of the answer
Notice that the answer denies necessity. It gives two possibilities: first, force may disappear completely upon division, just like the unity of the body.
3
Examine the second part of the answer
Even if force remains, it does not follow that it is sufficient for action. The example of ten people clarifies this: one alone may not move the stone at all.
The example shows that a part's force may be insufficient even if the whole's force was effective.
4
Reflect on the logical force
Consider how this answer undermines the objection. The objection assumed persistence and proportionality, but the answer shows these assumptions are unfounded.
This connects to the core idea: force is not automatically transferred.

( الثاني : أن النصف ) من الجسم ( له قوة مؤثرة وهو غير لازم؛ لجواز أن يكون لجسم قوة مؤثرة حالة فيه فإذا انقسم ذلك الجسم بنصفين انعدمت تلك القوة بالكلية، كما تنعدم وحدة ذلك الجسم بالتقسيم، فلا يكون لنصف الجسم قوة أصلاً، وإن فرض أن له قوة هي جزء لقوة الكل فليس يلزم أن يكون جزءاً لقوة قوية على الفعل، فإن عشرة مثلاً إذا أقلوا حجراً في مسافة، فالواحد منهم إذا انفرد ربما لا يقوى على إقلاله في عشر تلك المسافة، بل لا يقوى عل تحريكه أصلاً. ( الثالث : أنها أي قوة النصف نصف قوة الكل)، وهو أيضاً غير مسلم؛ لجواز تفاوت القوة في أجزاء الجسم، فلا يكون انقسامها على نسبة انقسام الجسم، وهذان قوله : ( فإن عشرة إلخ ) تنظير لا تمثيل وإلا فالواجب أن يقول : ربما لا يقوى على إقلال عشر ذلك الحجر. قوله : (أنها أي قوة النصف إلخ) أي النسبة بين القوتين كالنسبة بين الجسمين وهذه المقدمة مما يتوقف عليه الدليل المذكور إذ لولا ذلك لجاز أن تكون قوة النصف مثل قوة الكل فيكون لكل منهما آثار لا تتناهى فما قيل إن هذا المنع غير نافع إذ مجرد القول بحلول قوة في نصف الجسم سواء كانت نصف القوة الحالة في الكل أو لا كاف للمستدل إذ لا شك أن تلك القوة أقل من القوة الحالة في الكل والدليل ينتظم بمجرد ذلك على المطلوب وهم كما لا يخفى إذ الأقلية غير لازمة من الحلول في نصف الجسم، ولو سلم فمجرد الأقلية غير كافية. إذ ليس النسبة بين القوتين كالنسبة بين الجسمين فيجوز أن يكون آثار الأقل متناهية، وآثار الكل غير متناهية، فلا يلزم خلاف المفروض . قوله : ( وهذان الأمران) أي الثاني والثالث . المحل مشعر بأن الاستدلال بطريق التقسيم لكن الكلام في الاحتياج إليه هذا في القوة الطبيعية وأما في القوة القسرية فيقال يكفي قدرة ذلك القاسر على تحريك نصف ذلك الجسم ولا حاجة إلى إثبات قدرته على تحريك ضعفه فإن تحريك الكل إذا كان غير متناه يكون تحريك النصف أيضاً غير متناه مع أنه أزيد من تحريك الكل الذي هو الضعف ضرورة قلة المعاوق فيه من اتحاد القاسر فيقع الزيادة في الجهة التي هو فيها غير متناهية لاتحاد مبدأ الحركتين بالفرض فيلزم الانقطاع كما ذكر في الشرح. قوله : ( فإن عشرة مثلاً إذا أقلوا إلخ ) هذا طريق التمثيل والتوضيح للمنع السابق وإلا فلقائل أن يقول كلامنا في التحريك الطبيعي الذي لا معاوق فيه والواحد من العشرة في الصورة المذكورة إنما لا يقوى على إقلال ذلك الحجر بسبب المعاوقة التي لا يقاومها قوة الواحد فالقياس مع الفارق على أن اللازم من كون نسبة القوتين في التحريك الطبيعي على نسبة المحلين وتحريك القوتين جسمها لزوم تحريك واحدة من العشرة عشر ذلك الحجر لا كله اللهم إلا أن يقال فرض تحريك نصف قوة الكل باعتبار أنها إنما حلت فيه وإلا فلا فرق بين النصف والكل في قبول أصل الحركة بذلك القدر من القوة ولذا اعتبر في التمثيل انتفاء قدرة الواحد على تحريك كل الحجر في عشر تلك المسافة فتأمل بقي الكلام في جواز وجود القوة بدون تأثير ما وإن كان ضعيفاً. قوله : فلا يكون انقسامها على نسبة انقسام الجسم كون تفاوت القوتين على حسب تفاوت المحلين وإن فرض فيما مر إلا أن الظاهر أنه يكفي في الاستدلال كون نسبة نصف القوة إلى كلها في القلة بقدر متناه وإن لم يكن بالنصفية بعينها .
(The second: that the half) of the body (has an effective force, and that is not necessary; because it is possible that a body has an effective force residing in it, so when that body is divided into two halves, that force disappears entirely, just as the unity of that body disappears with division. Thus, the half of the body has no force at all. And if it is assumed that it has a force that is a part of the force of the whole, it does not follow that it is a part of a force strong enough for action. For example, ten people lifting a stone over a distance – one of them alone may not be able to lift it in a tenth of that distance, but may not be able to move it at all. (The third: that it, i.e., the force of the half, is half the force of the whole), and that is also not granted; because the force may vary among the parts of the body, so its division is not proportional to the division of the body. These two His statement: (for example, ten etc.) is an analogy, not an exact similitude, otherwise he should have said: may not be able to lift a tenth of that stone. His statement: (that it, i.e., the force of the half, etc.) – i.e., the ratio between the two forces is like the ratio between the two bodies, and this premise is something on which the mentioned proof depends. For if not, it would be possible that the force of the half is like the force of the whole, so each would have infinite effects. So what is said – that this prevention is not useful, because merely saying that a force resides in half the body, whether it is half the force in the whole or not, suffices for the arguer, since there is no doubt that that force is less than the force in the whole, and the proof is established by that on the desired conclusion – is an illusion, as is not hidden. For the lesser is not necessary from residing in half the body, and if granted, mere lessness is not sufficient. For the ratio between the two forces is not like the ratio between the two bodies, so it is possible that the effects of the lesser are finite, and the effects of the whole are infinite, so the contrary of the supposition does not follow. His statement: (and these two matters) i.e., the second and third. The place indicates that the inference is by way of division, but the discussion concerns the need for it. This is in the case of natural force. As for compulsory force, it is said: the ability of that compeller to move half of that body suffices, and there is no need to prove his ability to move its double. For if the movement of the whole is infinite, the movement of the half is also infinite, even though it is more than the movement of the whole which is the double, due to the necessary lack of resistance in it from the unity of the compeller. Thus the increase occurs in the direction in which it is infinite, due to the unity of the starting point of the two movements by assumption, so cessation follows, as mentioned in the commentary. His statement: (for example, ten, etc.) – this is a way of illustration and clarification of the previous prevention. Otherwise, one could say: our discourse is about natural motion in which there is no resistance, and the one individual among the ten in the mentioned scenario is unable to lift that stone only because of the resistance that the individual's force cannot overcome. So the analogy is with a difference. Moreover, the necessary consequence of the ratio of the two forces in natural motion being according to the ratio of the two loci and the motion of the two forces of their body is the necessity of the one of the ten moving a tenth of that stone, not the whole. Unless it is said: the assumption is the movement of half the force of the whole by virtue of its residing in it; otherwise, there is no difference between the half and the whole in accepting the original motion with that amount of force. Hence, the example considered the inability of the individual to move the whole stone in a tenth of that distance. So reflect. There remains the discussion about the permissibility of existence of force without any effect, even if weak. His statement: (thus its division is not proportional to the division of the body) – because the variation of the two forces is according to the variation of the two loci, even if it is assumed in what preceded except that it is apparent that in the inference it suffices that the ratio of half the force to the whole is finite in smallness, even if it is not exactly half.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and their answers regarding bodily force and division. The second objection states that the half of a body has an effective force, but the answer denies that this is necessary. It argues that when a body is divided, the force may disappear entirely, just as unity disappears. Even if a force remains in the half, it need not be part of a force strong enough for action, as illustrated by the example of ten people lifting a stone – one alone may not even move it. The third objection claims that the force of the half is half the force of the whole, but the answer denies this because force may vary among parts. The commentary then elaborates: the example is an analogy, not a perfect similitude. The premise that the forces are proportional is necessary for the proof; otherwise, the half could have the same force as the whole, leading to infinite effects. Some argue that simply positing a force in the half, even if less, suffices for the proof, but the commentary refutes this by noting that lessness is not guaranteed, and even if it is, the less force may have finite effects while the whole has infinite ones. The two matters (second and third) are treated together. The discussion then distinguishes between natural force (where division is relevant) and compulsory force (where the compeller's ability to move half suffices without needing to prove ability to move double). The commentary further clarifies the example of ten people: in natural motion without resistance, the ratio of forces should match the ratio of bodies, so one individual should move a tenth of the stone, not the whole. The passage concludes by noting that the inference only requires that the ratio of half to whole is finite in smallness, not necessarily exactly half.

النصف
الفعل
التقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that force is always proportional to the size of the body, so half the body must have half the force.
Correction: The passage shows that force may disappear entirely upon division, or if it remains, it may be insufficient for any effect, disproving automatic proportionality.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it not necessary that half a body has an effective force?
A) Because half a body cannot have any force.
B) Because the force may disappear upon division or remain insufficient.
C) Because the whole body's force is not real.
D) Because force only exists in living things.

Key Takeaway
Force does not necessarily persist or remain effective when a body is divided; the assumption of automatic transfer is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own abilities, do you assume that more effort automatically brings proportional results? This lesson shows that force can vanish upon division. In what area of your life have you placed too much trust in your own strength rather than in Allah? What will you do today to shift that trust?
?
Think of a group effort you have been part of—like a community project or family support. Do you ever compare your individual contribution to the whole and feel inadequate or self-sufficient? This lesson teaches that one person alone may not even produce a fraction of the group's effect. How does that change your view of working together for Allah's sake? Will you commit to joining a group effort this week, regardless of your perceived weakness?
?
The ten men could lift a stone together, but one alone could not move it at all. Is there a spiritual burden you are trying to carry alone—like a persistent sin, a difficult relationship, or a lack of knowledge? This lesson reminds you that force is not automatically divided. What step will you take today to seek help from others or from Allah, instead of assuming you can handle half?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
5 min
Third Objection: Force of Half is Half of Whole? A Denial
Sanusi denies the third objection and the commentary elaborates on the necessity of proportionality and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the force of a half is exactly half the force of the whole is not necessarily true, and how this affects the proof of infinite effects.

Core Idea
Force may vary among parts, so the ratio of forces is not necessarily proportional to the bodies' sizes.

The third objection claims that the force of the half is exactly half the force of the whole, which would support the argument for infinite effects. Sanusi denies this premise, arguing that force can vary among parts. The commentary deepens the discussion, addressing the necessity of proportionality and distinguishing between natural and compulsory force. Let's examine the objection, the denial, and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its denial
Read the Arabic passage for the third objection and the immediate denial. Note that the denial says the claim is not granted because force may vary.
2
Understand the importance of proportionality
The commentary explains that the proof relies on the premise that the forces are proportional to the bodies. Without it, the half could have the same force as the whole, leading to infinite effects.
This shows why the objection matters.
3
Analyze the refutation of the alternative argument
Some argue that even without exact half, any lesser force suffices. The commentary refutes this: lessness is not guaranteed, and even if it is, the lesser force could have finite effects while the whole has infinite ones.
4
Distinguish between natural and compulsory force
The commentary notes that for natural force, division is critical; for compulsory force, only the ability to move half matters without needing to prove ability to move double.
This shows the scope of the argument.

Commentary

The second objection states that the half of a body has an effective force, but the answer denies that this is necessary. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a whole body has infinite effects, then its half must also have infinite effects because half is a part.
Correction: The passage shows that force may not be proportional, so half could have finite effects even if the whole has infinite effects.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the claim that the force of the half is exactly half the force of the whole not necessarily true?
A) Because half cannot have force.
B) Because force may vary among parts of the body.
C) Because the whole has no force.
D) Because the half always has more force.

Key Takeaway
Force is not necessarily proportional to body size; the assumption of exact half force is unfounded, which undermines arguments that rely on such proportionality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe your abilities are determined by your physical or social standing—like the half has half the whole's force. But what if God can grant you the same effectiveness as someone with 'more'? Where in your life have you limited yourself by assuming proportionality? Will you trust that His command over means is not bound by your measurements?
?
When you feel your resources are only half of what you need, do you automatically assume your outcome will be half as good? The lesson shows that force need not be proportional. What one area of your life today can you surrender to Allah knowing He can produce full effect from small means? What holds you back from acting on that?
?
The natural versus compulsory force distinction mirrors reliance on self versus reliance on Allah. Which force do you operate from in your daily struggles? Will you examine your actions to see if you are trusting in proportional natural causes or in the unbounded power of the One who moves without measure?

Conditions for the Proof of Finitude of Natural Power
Al-Mawaqif states the two conditions for the proof of finitude of natural power and gives examples and a limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conditions that limit the application of the proof of finitude of natural power and how it relates to natural motions including those from souls.

Core Idea
The proof of finitude of natural power only applies to uniform powers without resistance.

Al-Mawaqif draws a careful boundary around the proof of finitude of natural power. Not every power in bodies can be proven finite through this argument. The author specifies two key conditions and gives examples to clarify the scope. This lesson will unpack these conditions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two conditions
Identify the two conditions mentioned: (1) power is in a body with no resistance, (2) power is divisible uniformly with the body. These are the prerequisites for the proof.
The Arabic phrase 'لا معاوقة' means 'no resistance' and 'على التشابه' means 'uniformly'.
2
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples: natures in elemental bodies and impressed souls in celestial bodies. Reflect on why these fit the conditions of no resistance and uniform divisibility.
Elemental bodies like earth, water, air, fire are considered uniform in their natures.
3
Consider the limitation with soul motions
The passage notes that natural motion, as opposed to forced motion, also includes motions from souls. This suggests that motions emanating from souls may not meet the conditions, thus limiting the proof's scope.
This shows the proof is not universal for all natural motions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمران معتبران في برهان تناهي القوة الطبيعية، ولهذا قيل : إن هذا البرهان إنما يجري في قوة حالة في جسم لا معاوقة فيه منقسمة بانقسام ذلك الجسم على التشابه كالطبائع في الأجسام العنصرية وكالنفوس المنطبعة في الأجرام الفلكية، لكن التحريك الطبيعي المقابل للتحريك القسري يتناول أيضاً التحريك الصادر عن النفوس
The two matters are considered in the proof of the finitude of natural power. For this it was said: This proof only runs in a power residing in a body in which there is no resistance, divisible by the division of that body uniformly, like the natures in elemental bodies and like the impressed souls in celestial bodies. But natural motion, as opposed to forced motion, also includes motion emanating from souls.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of the proof of finitude of natural power. It states that two conditions (الأمران) are taken into account in this proof, and therefore it was said that the proof only applies to a power that resides in a body with no resistance, is divisible by the division of that body, and is uniform (on similarity, على التشابه). Examples are given: natures in elemental bodies and impressed souls in celestial bodies. However, natural motion, which is contrasted with forced motion, also includes motion emanating from souls (النفوس) — which may not conform to these conditions, thus presenting a limitation of the proof.

Key Terms
برهان تناهي القوة الطبيعية
لا معاوقة
على التشابه
النفوس المنطبعة
التحريك الطبيعي
التحريك القسري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of finitude of natural power applies to all natural motions.
Correction: The proof only applies to powers that are in a body without resistance and divisible uniformly, not to motions emanating from souls.

Checkpoint
What are the two conditions (الأمران) for the proof of finitude of natural power according to this passage?
A) The power must be in a body without resistance and be divisible uniformly.
B) The power must be infinite and non-material.
C) The power must be from a soul and not from nature.
D) The power must be coercive and not natural.

Key Takeaway
The proof of finitude of natural power is restricted to powers that are in a body without resistance and divisible uniformly, and does not automatically cover all natural motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life do you assume that your efforts will succeed because you have 'natural power', without acknowledging the resistance of your own desires or external trials? How can you honestly assess the conditions under which your intentions bear fruit?
?
The proof requires uniformity and no resistance. Are there areas of your faith—like prayer or charity—where your performance is inconsistent or hindered by inner resistance? What change must you make to align with the condition of uniformity in devotion?

Avicenna's Defense of the Infinite Power Proof
Avicenna presents his reply to the objection about infinite power being only for the whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Avicenna's attempt to refute the objection that infinite coercive power belongs only to the whole body, not its parts.

Core Idea
Avicenna argues that power pervades the whole, so parts share in it proportional to size.

In this passage, the commentator presents Avicenna's (al-Shaykh) effort to defend the proof of infinite coercive power against a key objection. The objector claims that infinite power is only for the whole body, not for its parts, and thus the proof fails. Avicenna's reply introduces the concept of pervasiveness (sariya) to argue that the power is distributed throughout the body, allowing proportional strength in each part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objector's claim: infinite power exists only for the whole body; if divided, parts get no power. Note how the objector uses the ship mover analogy.
This objection challenges the very foundation of the proof.
2
Understand Avicenna's reply
Avicenna replies that power is pervasive (sāriya) in the entire body, so parts do have some power. He argues this using the examples of composite mixtures and ship movers.
The term sāriya is central—it means flowing or permeating.
3
Grasp the estimation method
Avicenna says we can consider a part without separating it, like in engineering estimations, and determine that the part's power can move something smaller.
This is a hypothetical reasoning—not a claim about actual division.
4
Note the commentator's framing
The commentator introduces Avicenna's argument as 'strained' (tamahhala), signaling that it is not convincing.
This hints at the coming refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (معتبران إلخ بخلاف برهان لاتناهي القوة القسرية فإن الجسمين المتناسبين بالضعفية والنصفية موجودان والقوتان على التناسب المذكور متحققتان فيهما، فلا حاجة في ذلك البرهان إلى هذين الأمرين اعلم أن الشيخ تمحل في الشفاء لدفع هذه المنوع، فقال : ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه يجوز أن تكون هذه القوة الغير المتناهية إنما توجد لجملة الجسم، فإذا قسم الجسم بطلت فلم توجد من تلك القوة شيء للجزء، فلم يقو الجزء على شيء مما يقوى عليه الكل؛ لأن كل هذه القوة للكل كما يوجد من القوى في الأجسام المركبة بعد المزاج، ولا تكون موجودة لشيء من الأركان التي امتزجت عنها ، وكما أن المحركين للسفينة، فإن الواحد منهم لا يحركها البتة، فنقول : إن الأمر ليس كما قررتم فإن القوة وإن كانت للجسم بحال اجتماع أجزائه، وبحال مزاجه، فإنها مع ذلك تكون سارية في جملته، وإلا لكانت قوة لبعض الجملة دون الكل، وإذا كانت سارية في جملته كان لبعضها بعض القوة فيكون البسيط إذن في حال المزاج حاملاً للقوة الحاصلة بعد المزاج السارية في الكل. وإنما يحملها في حال الإنفراد؛ إذ ليس يجب أن يكون فرضنا الجسم بعضاً يلجئنا إلى أن نأخذ ذلك البعض بشرط قطعه وإبانته حتى يكون لقائل أن يقول : إن البعض المبان لا يحمل من القوة شيئاً، بل يكفينا أن نعين بعضاً منه وهو بحاله فنتعرف حال ما يصدر عن ذلك البعض عن القوة التي فيه وحدها التعرف المفروغ سبيل التقدير والمحركون للسفينة فإن الواحد منهم وإن لم يمكن أن يحرك كل السفينة فيمكن أن يحرك أصغر منه لا محالة ويلزم ما قلنا انتهى ولا يخفى ما فيه لأنا لا نسلم كون القوة سارية في جملته قوله : وإلا لكانت قوة لبعض الجملة دون الكل ممنوع لجواز حلوله في الكل من حيث هو دون شيء من أجزائه ولو سلم كونها سارية فيه فلا نسلم الملازمة المستفادة من قوله وإذا كانت سارية في جملته كان لبعضها بعض القوة، إذ لا يلزم أن يكون بعض القوة قوة، ولو سلم ذلك لا يلزم أن تكون القوتان على تناسب الجسمين فالمنوع المذكورة واردة على هذا التقدير أيضاً، أعني اعتبار البعض متصلاً بالكل، وبناء البرهان على تقدير هذه الأمور كتقديرات المهندسين في عدم وجودها بالفعل لأنا نمنع إمكان هذه الأمور في نفس الأمر ومجرد الفرض لا يجدي نفعا
His statement: (Both matters etc. — unlike the proof of the infinitude of coercive power, for the two bodies proportional in weakness and half exist, and the two powers on the mentioned proportion are actualized in them, so there is no need in that proof for these two matters. Know that the Master (al-Shaykh) strained in the Shifa' to repel this objection, and said: Then an objector may say that it is possible that this infinite power only exists for the whole body, so if the body is divided it ceases, and none of that power exists for the part, so the part has no strength for anything the whole has strength for, because all this power is for the whole, just as powers exist in composite bodies after mixture, and do not exist for any of the elements that were mixed from it, and like the movers of a ship, for one of them does not move it at all. So we say: The matter is not as you have stated, for the power, even if it belongs to the body in the state of the gathering of its parts and in the state of its mixture, nonetheless it is pervasive (sāriya) in its entirety; otherwise it would be a power for a part of the whole and not for the whole. And if it is pervasive in its entirety, then some of it has some power, so the simple, then, in the state of mixture carries the power obtained after the mixture pervasive in the whole. It only carries it in the state of separation; for it is not necessary that our supposing the body a part forces us to take that part under condition of cutting it off and separating it, so that an objector could say that the separated part carries none of the power. Rather, it suffices for us to specify a part of it while it is in its state, and we will know the state of what emanates from that part from the power in it alone — a knowledge accepted in the way of estimation. And the movers of a ship, for one of them, even if he cannot move the whole ship, can move something smaller than it necessarily. And what we said follows. End. And it is not hidden what is in it, for we do not grant that the power is pervasive in its entirety. His statement: 'Otherwise it would be a power for a part of the whole and not for the whole' is denied, for it is possible that it resides in the whole as such without any of its parts. And even if we grant that it is pervasive in it, we do not grant the entailment derived from his statement: 'And if it is pervasive in its entirety, then some of it has some power', for it does not follow that some power is (a divisible) power. And even if that is granted, it does not follow that the two powers are proportional to the two bodies. So the mentioned objections apply to this estimation as well, i.e., considering the part connected to the whole, and building the proof on the estimation of these matters like the estimations of engineers in their non-existence in actuality, for we deny the possibility of these matters in themselves, and mere supposition is of no benefit.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the original text. It first explains that the two conditions are not needed in the proof of infinite coercive power because the proportional bodies exist. Then it discusses Avicenna's (الشيخ) strained attempt to refute an objection about infinite power only existing for the whole body. The objector says if the power is only for the whole, then when divided, the part has no power. Avicenna's reply argues that the power is pervasive (سارية) in the whole, so parts have some power, proportional. The commentator then refutes: (1) He does not grant that the power is pervasive; it could reside in the whole as such without being in its parts. (2) Even if pervasive, it does not follow that 'some of the power' is a power (i.e., that a part of the power is itself a power). (3) Even if that were granted, it does not follow that the powers are proportional to the bodies. (4) The whole estimation is like engineers' hypothetical constructions, not actual. Hence the objection stands.

Key Terms
برهان لاتناهي القوة القسرية
سارية
الشيخ
الشفاء
المزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Avicenna's pervasiveness argument successfully saves the proof without further criticism.
Correction: The commentator will later show that the argument is unsound by denying pervasiveness and its logical implications.

Checkpoint
According to Avicenna's reply, what does it mean for the power to be 'sāriya'?
A) The power is only for the whole body, not the parts.
B) The power is distributed throughout the entire body, so parts share in it.
C) The power is created anew in each part after division.
D) The power is indivisible and remains only in the whole.

Key Takeaway
Avicenna defends the infinite power proof by asserting that power is pervasive in the whole body, allowing parts to have proportional strength even without actual separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the vastness of creation, do you truly believe that God's power is present in every part? What would change in your prayer if you truly felt His power pervasive?
?
Avicenna used the ship analogy: one mover cannot move the whole ship but can move a smaller part. In your life, have you ever assumed you cannot make a difference because you are only a part? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
The objection says power only belongs to the whole, not parts. Reflect on a time you felt powerless as an individual. How does Avicenna's reply that the part still carries some power give you hope in your relationship with God?

The Commentator's Refutation of Avicenna's Pervasiveness Argument
The commentator refutes Avicenna's pervasiveness argument point by point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's four-pronged refutation of Avicenna's claim that power is pervasive in the whole body.

Core Idea
The commentator denies pervasiveness and its implications, showing Avicenna's estimation is baseless.

After presenting Avicenna's defense, the commentator now launches a detailed refutation. He systematically denies each step: first, that power is pervasive at all; second, that if it were pervasive, it follows that a part has some power; third, that the powers are proportional; and finally, that the whole estimation is merely hypothetical like engineers' suppositions, not actual. Thus the original objection stands.

Guided Path
1
Denial of pervasiveness
The commentator first denies that the power is necessarily pervasive. It could reside in the whole without being in any part.
This is the key point—if pervasiveness is not granted, the rest collapses.
2
Denial of entailment to 'some power'
Even if pervasive, having 'some of the power' does not mean having a power; the power might not be divisible into sub-powers.
The commentator distinguishes between 'some of the power' and 'a power'.
3
Denial of proportionality
Even if a part has some power, it does not follow that the powers of different bodies are proportional to their sizes.
This blocks Avicenna's conclusion.
4
Denial of validity of estimation
The whole argument is based on mere hypothetical estimation, like engineers' abstract calculations, not actual existence. The commentator denies that such possibilities are actual.
Mere supposition has no evidential force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (معتبران إلخ بخلاف برهان لاتناهي القوة القسرية فإن الجسمين المتناسبين بالضعفية والنصفية موجودان والقوتان على التناسب المذكور متحققتان فيهما، فلا حاجة في ذلك البرهان إلى هذين الأمرين اعلم أن الشيخ تمحل في الشفاء لدفع هذه المنوع، فقال : ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه يجوز أن تكون هذه القوة الغير المتناهية إنما توجد لجملة الجسم، فإذا قسم الجسم بطلت فلم توجد من تلك القوة شيء للجزء، فلم يقو الجزء على شيء مما يقوى عليه الكل؛ لأن كل هذه القوة للكل كما يوجد من القوى في الأجسام المركبة بعد المزاج، ولا تكون موجودة لشيء من الأركان التي امتزجت عنها ، وكما أن المحركين للسفينة، فإن الواحد منهم لا يحركها البتة، فنقول : إن الأمر ليس كما قررتم فإن القوة وإن كانت للجسم بحال اجتماع أجزائه، وبحال مزاجه، فإنها مع ذلك تكون سارية في جملته، وإلا لكانت قوة لبعض الجملة دون الكل، وإذا كانت سارية في جملته كان لبعضها بعض القوة فيكون البسيط إذن في حال المزاج حاملاً للقوة الحاصلة بعد المزاج السارية في الكل. وإنما يحملها في حال الإنفراد؛ إذ ليس يجب أن يكون فرضنا الجسم بعضاً يلجئنا إلى أن نأخذ ذلك البعض بشرط قطعه وإبانته حتى يكون لقائل أن يقول : إن البعض المبان لا يحمل من القوة شيئاً، بل يكفينا أن نعين بعضاً منه وهو بحاله فنتعرف حال ما يصدر عن ذلك البعض عن القوة التي فيه وحدها التعرف المفروغ سبيل التقدير والمحركون للسفينة فإن الواحد منهم وإن لم يمكن أن يحرك كل السفينة فيمكن أن يحرك أصغر منه لا محالة ويلزم ما قلنا انتهى ولا يخفى ما فيه لأنا لا نسلم كون القوة سارية في جملته قوله : وإلا لكانت قوة لبعض الجملة دون الكل ممنوع لجواز حلوله في الكل من حيث هو دون شيء من أجزائه ولو سلم كونها سارية فيه فلا نسلم الملازمة المستفادة من قوله وإذا كانت سارية في جملته كان لبعضها بعض القوة، إذ لا يلزم أن يكون بعض القوة قوة، ولو سلم ذلك لا يلزم أن تكون القوتان على تناسب الجسمين فالمنوع المذكورة واردة على هذا التقدير أيضاً، أعني اعتبار البعض متصلاً بالكل، وبناء البرهان على تقدير هذه الأمور كتقديرات المهندسين في عدم وجودها بالفعل لأنا نمنع إمكان هذه الأمور في نفس الأمر ومجرد الفرض لا يجدي نفعا

Know that the Master (al-Shaykh) strained in the Shifa' to repel this objection, and said: Then an objector may say that it is possible that this infinite power only exists for the whole body, so if the body is divided it ceases, and none of that power exists for the part, so the part has no strength for anything the whole has strength for, because all this power is for the whole, just as powers exist in composite bodies after mixture, and do not exist for any of the elements that were mixed from it, and like the movers of a ship, for one of them does not move it at all. And if it is pervasive in its entirety, then some of it has some power, so the simple, then, in the state of mixture carries the power obtained after the mixture pervasive in the whole. It only carries it in the state of separation; for it is not necessary that our supposing the body a part forces us to take that part under condition of cutting it off and separating it, so that an objector could say that the separated part carries none of the power. Rather, it suffices for us to specify a part of it while it is in its state, and we will know the state of what emanates from that part from the power in it alone — a knowledge accepted in the way of estimation. And the movers of a ship, for one of them, even if he cannot move the whole ship, can move something smaller than it necessarily. And what we said follows. End. This passage is a commentary on the original text. It first explains that the two conditions are not needed in the proof of infinite coercive power because the proportional bodies exist. Then it discusses Avicenna's (الشيخ) strained attempt to refute an objection about infinite power only existing for the whole body. (3) Even if that were granted, it does not follow that the powers are proportional to the bodies. (4) The whole estimation is like engineers' hypothetical constructions, not actual. What is the commentator's first reason for rejecting Avicenna's claim?
A) The power is not infinite.

?
In which areas of your own beliefs do you rely on assumptions or hypothetical scenarios that have no basis in reality, like the engineers' estimations? How can you ground your convictions in what is actual and certain?
?

?
If you cannot intellectually dismantle an argument against your faith, are you willing to stand firm on the basis that mere possibility does not equal actuality? How does this lesson challenge you to defend your beliefs with both reason and reliance on divine truth?

The Objection from Indivisibility and Resistances
Al-Mawaqif states the objection with two reasons and a conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof of finitude from natural power does not apply to most plant and animal souls due to indivisibility and composite resistances.

Core Idea
Indivisibility and resistances invalidate the proof for most souls.

In this lesson, we examine the objection raised by al-Mawaqif against the philosophers' proof of finitude. The objection points out that most plant and animal souls are not divisible by the division of their places, and their bodies contain resistances. Let's read the original text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice the two reasons given for the objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the sentence beginning with 'مع أن' (first reason: indivisibility) and the sentence beginning with 'وأيضاً' (second reason: resistances).
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is 'فلا يصح أن حركة الكل ضعف حركة النصف'. See how the two reasons lead to this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في القوة الجسمانية النباتية والحيوانية، مع أن أكثر تلك النفوس لا تنقسم بانقسام محالها . وأيضاً أجسام النباتات والحيوانات مركبة من بسائط لا تخلو من معاوقات تقتضيها طبائعها، فيقع التفاوت في التحريك الطبيعي الصادر عن تلك النفوس بسبب تلك المعاوقات الحاصلة في القابل المركب، فلا يصح أن حركة الكل ضعف حركة النصف . قوله : (مع أن أكثر تلك النفوس إلخ) لكون تلك المحال أجساماً آلية وإنما قال أكثر لأن بعض النفوس النباتية تكون منقسمة بانقسام المحل ولذا يبقى النامية والغاذية والمولدة في أغصان بعض الأشجار بعد انفصالها عنها . قوله : ( وأيضاً أجسام إلخ ) بيان لفائدة التقييد بقوله لا معاوقة فيه . قوله فلا يصح إلخ) لأن قوة الكل وإن فرض قوة النصف لكل معاوق الكل أكثر من نصف معاوق النصف فيجوز أن يحصل التعادل بين القوتين ويكون آثار كليهما غير متناهية
The Fifth Observation - The Fifth Objective: The Philosophers' Saying about Bodily Power (the plant and animal [souls]), given that most of those souls are not divided by the division of their places. Also, the bodies of plants and animals are composed of simples that are not free from resistances necessitated by their natures, so disparity occurs in the natural motion emanating from those souls due to those resistances occurring in the composite recipient. Therefore, it is not correct that the motion of the whole is double the motion of the half. His statement: (Given that most of those souls etc.) because those places are instrumental bodies; he only said 'most' because some plant souls are divisible by the division of the place, and hence the nutritive, vegetative, and generative souls remain in the branches of some trees after their separation from them. His statement: (And also bodies etc.) is an explanation of the benefit of the restriction by his saying: 'no resistance in it'. His statement: (So it is not correct etc.) because the power of the whole, even if supposed to be double the power of the half, each of the whole's resistances is more than half of the half's resistances, so it is possible that equilibrium occurs between the two powers and the effects of both are infinite.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof of finitude of natural power. It first states the original text from al-Mawaqif: the proof is applied to plant and animal souls, but most of these souls are not divisible by the division of their bodies (places), and their bodies are composed of simples that have inherent resistances. Therefore, there is variation in the natural motion from these souls, so the motion of the whole is not necessarily double the motion of the half. The commentator then explains: (1) 'Most' is used because some plant souls are divisible, as seen in the nutrient, growth, and generative souls remaining in separated branches. (2) The restriction 'no resistance' in the original proof is highlighted: composite bodies have resistances. (3) Even if the whole's power were double the half's, the whole's resistance is more than half of the half's resistance, so it is possible that the two powers are balanced and both produce infinite effects, thus making the proof invalid.

النفوس الحيوانية
معاوقات
القابل المركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of finitude applies to all natural powers uniformly.
Correction: This objection shows it fails for most plant and animal souls due to indivisibility and resistances.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does the proof of finitude not apply to most plant and animal souls?
A) Because they are immortal.
B) Because they are not divisible with their places and have resistances.
C) Because they are simple substances.
D) Because they are not created.

Key Takeaway
The proof of finitude fails for most souls because they are indivisible and their bodies contain resistances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This objection shows that the philosophers' proof fails because it ignores resistances in composites. In your own spiritual life, are you ignoring 'resistances'—habits, attachments, or distractions—that prevent your good intentions from being realized? What specific resistance will you address today?
?
The indivisibility of the soul is used here as a reason to reject a worldly proof. Does your belief in the indivisibility of your own soul change how you view your identity beyond your physical body? What would it mean for you to live as if your soul is not confined to your body's limits?

The Exception of Divisible Plant Souls
The commentator clarifies the restriction 'most' with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author said 'most' and not 'all', and how some plant souls are divisible.

Core Idea
Some plant souls are divisible, as seen in separated branches.

The objector says 'most' souls are not divisible. The commentator explains that this is because some plant souls are actually divisible, unlike the majority. This lesson explores the term 'most' and the example of the nutritive, growth, and generative souls persisting in detached branches.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

2
Understand the reason for 'most'
The commentator says 'أكثر' is used because some plant souls are divisible by the division of the place.
3
Note the example
The example is that the nutritive, growth, and generative souls remain in branches after separation, showing divisibility.

The Role of Resistances and the Possibility of Equilibrium
The commentator explains the significance of the no-resistance restriction and the equilibrium possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how resistances in composite bodies can negate the proportionality of motion and allow for infinite effects.

Core Idea
Resistances can balance powers, making infinite effects possible.

The objection's second reason involves resistances. The commentator elaborates: even if the whole has twice the power of the half, its resistances are also more than half the half's resistances, so equilibrium is possible. This lesson dives into that analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the commentator's explanation of the restriction 'لا معاوقة' and the equilibrium argument.
2
Understand the significance of 'no resistance'
The commentator says this restriction is important: the proof only works if there is no resistance, but composite bodies have resistances.
3
Grasp the equilibrium argument
Even if whole power is double half power, whole resistances are more than half resistances, so equilibrium can occur, making both effects infinite.
